        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202305686')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="f6b08a1821dc492097cb665c4daa750d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="f6b08a1821dc492097cb665c4daa750d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd7b6159e-6675-47e4-b227-a082d4f81053'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '5075509F03268DBB5430078CD494DCE8';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d995782" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Highly porous carbon materials with a rationally designed pore structure can be utilized as reservoirs for metal or nonmetal components. The use of small-sized metal or metal compound nanoparticles, completely encapsulated by carbon materials, has attracted significant attention as an effective approach to enhancing sodium ion storage properties. These materials have the ability to mitigate structural collapse caused by volume expansion during the charging process, enable short ion transport length, and prevent polysulfide elution. In this study, a concept of highly porous carbon-coated carbon nanotube (CNT) porous microspheres, which serve as excellent reservoir materials is suggested and a porous microsphere is developed by encapsulating iron sulfide nanocrystals within the highly porous carbon-coated CNTs using a sulfidation process. Furthermore, various sulfidation processes to determine the optimal method for achieving complete encapsulation are investigated by comparing the morphologies of diverse iron sulfide-carbon composites. The fully encapsulated structure, combined with the porous carbon, provides ample space to accommodate the significant volume changes during cycling. As a result, the porous iron sulfide-carbon-CNT composite microspheres exhibited outstanding cycling stability (293&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 600 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and remarkable rate capability (100&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305686-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305686-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Highly porous carbon materials with a nanostructured pore architecture have been utilized as reservoirs for metal or nonmetal components.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0001_R_d995774e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Small">1</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0005_R_d995774e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Commun.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The developed micro- and mesoporous voids in carbon materials can incorporate small metal or metal compound nanocrystals through an infiltration process at liquified states. By embedding the metal or metal compound nanocrystals in small pores and subjecting them to a sintering heat treatment, the crystal growth can be effectively suppressed due to the favorable growth conditions. The utilization of small-sized metal or metal compound nanoparticles has garnered significant attention in various fields such as energy storage, catalysis, separation, and optoelectronics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0006_R_d995774e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0009_R_d995774e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Arabian J. Chem.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the infiltration of small-sized nonmetals into porous carbon materials has also gained considerable interest, thanks to their enhanced reactivity and inhibition of elution, for example, lithium polychalcogenides (sulfide or selenide).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0010_R_d995774e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0012_R_d995774e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nanomaterials">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Nanostructured carbon materials containing ultrafine metal, metal compounds, or nonmetal components have the potential to greatly influence the electrical conductivity and structural flexibility of electrode materials used in rechargeable batteries. Among these materials, porous nanostructures are particularly noteworthy for energy storage applications due to their ability to mitigate structural collapse caused by volume expansion during the charging process, their short ion transport length, and their rapid electrolyte penetration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0013_R_d995774e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Eng. J.">13</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0018_R_d995774e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The shape of the carbon materials, along with the incorporation of enhanced conductive carbon composites, plays a vital role in developing electrode materials with improved electrochemical properties. One notable example is the utilization of one-dimensional (1D) carbon nanotubes (CNTs) as conductive agents in combination with various transition metal compounds. Additionally, three-dimensional (3D) CNT architectures composed of interconnected 1D CNTs with incorporated metal compounds have been extensively studied as efficient anode materials for alkali-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0019_R_d995774e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small">19</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0020_R_d995774e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Alloys Compd.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Compared to lithium-ion batteries, the electrochemical characteristics of sodium-ion batteries, such as specific capacity, cycling lifespan, and rate capability, are primarily determined by the small size and encapsulated position of the active materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0021_R_d995774e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small Methods">21</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0023_R_d995774e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Alloys Compd.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Due to the significant volume change and slow ion mobility during sodiation and desodiation processes, the use of ultrafine metal compound nanocrystals, completely encapsulated by carbon components, has been proposed as an effective strategy for enhancing the sodium ion storage properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0024_R_d995774e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J.">24</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0026_R_d995774e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, nanostructured carbon materials containing ultrafine metal compound nanocrystals emerge as promising candidates capable of delivering superior electrochemical performance as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, we propose a concept of highly porous carbon-coated CNT porous microspheres, which serve as excellent reservoir materials. The precursor microspheres, containing tin, magnesium, PVP, and sucrose-derived carbon, were initially prepared through spray pyrolysis. These precursor microspheres were then transformed into highly porous carbon-coated CNT porous microspheres by eliminating the tin and magnesium components using selenium gas. The core and shell of the microspheres were coated with PVP and sucrose, respectively, creating impregnation spaces for metal and nonmetal salts. Additionally, the porosity of the yolk part was controlled by adjusting the amount of sucrose, enabling the formation of either porous or dense microstructures. Subsequently, a porous microsphere was developed by encapsulating iron sulfide nanocrystals within the highly porous carbon-coated CNTs using a sulfidation process. Special attention was given to exploring suitable sulfidation methods to achieve complete encapsulation of the iron sulfide nanocrystals. The resulting electrode materials were then evaluated for their potential as anode materials in sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305686-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305686-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The formation mechanism of the highly porous carbon-coated CNT porous microspheres is visually depicted in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0010">1</a>. The spray pyrolysis process serves as the basis for the starting precursor material. In Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0010">1a</a>, the droplet containing tin, magnesium salt, acid-treated CNTs, sucrose, and polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP) undergoes drying and decomposition during spray pyrolysis, resulting in the formation of SnO<i><sub>x</sub></i>-MgO-C-CNT composite microspheres. Each component (SnO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, MgO nanocrystals, and CNTs) is uniformly compounded on a nanoscale inside the microspheres. However, PVP and sucrose, as carbon sources, exhibit partial phase segregation during the spray pyrolysis, as reported in previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0010_R_d995774e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0021_R_d995774e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small Methods">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The prepared precursor (SnO<i><sub>x</sub></i>-MgO-C-CNT composite) is then transformed into a porous carbon-coated CNT microsphere containing magnesium selenide nanocrystals through the elimination of tin components during the selenization process. Finally, the porous carbon-coated CNT porous microspheres (denoted as P-C-CNT) are obtained by removing the magnesium selenide nanocrystals through an acid-based etching process. The amount of sucrose in the spray solution determined the porous or dense shape of the carbon microsphere. In specific, the presence of low sucrose concentration (denoted as sucrose-low in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0010">1a</a>) led to significant crystal growth of tin selenide due to a small amount of sucrose-derived carbon, resulting in the formation of porous internal voids via the evaporation process of tin selenide. In Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0010">1b,c</a>, increased amounts of sucrose (denoted as sucrose-high in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0010">1b</a>) in the spray solution are applied, leading to the formation of a densely structured core part. During the selenization process, the carbon derived from sucrose surrounds the tin selenide and magnesium selenide nanocrystals and fills the core part. By etching the metal selenide nanocrystals, porous carbon-coated CNT dense microspheres (denoted as D-C-CNT) and dense carbon microspheres (denoted as D-C) are produced as comparative samples, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0010"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5f2f4f8e-5040-442a-9da5-c950de83de7a/smll202305686-fig-0010-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5f2f4f8e-5040-442a-9da5-c950de83de7a/smll202305686-fig-0010-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/09e8e09a-d427-48e4-93f6-8694c7e9d8aa/smll202305686-fig-0010-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5f2f4f8e-5040-442a-9da5-c950de83de7a/smll202305686-fig-0010-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0010&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of formation mechanisms for a) porous carbon-coated CNT porous microsphere, b) porous carbon-coated CNT dense microsphere, and c) dense carbon microsphere with different sucrose content and presence or absence of CNT component. The sucrose contents were calculated from PVP/sucrose weight ratio, where the ratio of 1/2 corresponds to sucrose-low and the ratio of 1/8 corresponds to sucrose-high, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphological characteristics of the sprayed precursor powders are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information, showcasing the differences based on the presence or absence of CNT and the amount of sucrose. In all the precursor particles, highly uniform composites of SnO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, MgO nanocrystals, and carbon sources are observed on a nanoscale within the microspheres. However, during the spray pyrolysis, phase separation occurs between PVP and sucrose-derived carbons. PVP migrates towards the outer shell, while sucrose assembles in the inner core, resulting in the formation of core-shell structured microspheres. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a>, Supporting Information, clearly depicts the core-shell structure of SnO<i><sub>x</sub></i>-MgO-C-CNT when an appropriate amount of sucrose is applied, which is further supported by TEM images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information. However, as the amount of sucrose increases, leading to core filling, a dense structured core shape is observed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c,d</a>, Supporting Information. In the absence of CNT in the spray solution, microspheres with smooth surfaces are observed as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1e,f</a>, Supporting Information. All three precursor powders exhibit low crystallinity due to the short-time pyrolysis during the spray process, as shown in XRD data (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The morphological properties of pure carbon microspheres, including P-C-CNT, D-C-CNT, and D-C with different inner spaces and types, were obtained by removing tin selenide and magnesium selenide nanocrystals from the spray precursor. The morphological characteristics of these microspheres are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information. Low-magnification SEM images of the three samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1a</a>, and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c</a>, Supporting Information) reveal spherical morphologies, and the slightly irregular particle size distribution is attributed to the formation of one microsphere from one droplet, which varies in size due to the spray generator. P-C-CNT microspheres exhibit short CNT threads on their surfaces, while the other samples have smooth surfaces. SEM images of broken carbon microspheres (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1a</a>, and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4d</a>, Supporting Information) provide insights into their spatial structures. TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1b,c</a>) indicate that P-C-CNT microspheres possess a porous internal structure with multiple voids. The mean size of the pores was calculated as about 140&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1c</a>). The highly porous carbon material is uniformly coated on the CNTs, providing ample space for infiltrating solutions containing metal and nonmetal salts, as indicated by the arrows in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1d</a>. The shell of the microsphere primarily consists of carbon derived from PVP due to the phase segregation during the spray pyrolysis, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1d</a>. Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns and elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0001">1e,f</a>) confirm the presence of pure carbon and complete elimination of Sn and Mg components. The complete removal of Sn and Mg components was further confirmed by EDS data in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information. Conversely, SEM images of broken D-C-CNT and D-C microspheres (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4d</a>, Supporting Information) reveal a densely structured core part resulting from increased amounts of sucrose. XRD patterns of the three carbon microspheres confirm the formation of pure carbon components after the complete etching process of tin and magnesium selenides, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface areas and pore structures of the carbon microspheres were analyzed through N<sub>2</sub> gas adsorption and desorption isotherms, as well as Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) measurement (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The BET surface areas of P-C-CNT, D-C-CNT, and D-C were 1452, 1022, and 1019 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with corresponding pore volumes of 0.90, 0.58, and 0.55 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. The removal of metal selenide nanocrystals and carbonization of carbon sources (sucrose and PVP) contribute to the high specific surface area of the carbon microspheres.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0021_R_d995774e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small Methods">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among them, P-C-CNT exhibits the highest surface area and pore volume due to its porous structure and etching of small-sized metal compounds. It is worth noting that the pore volume of micro- and mesopores decreases as the sucrose content increases. Additionally, the developed pore diameter of ≈4.3&nbsp;nm in P-C-CNT and D-C-CNT is not observed in D-C, indicating a greater development of mesopores due to the complexation with CNTs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/41957dd4-f935-4bb8-abdf-6af1d492f4a1/smll202305686-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/41957dd4-f935-4bb8-abdf-6af1d492f4a1/smll202305686-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/23b319c3-9827-4ff7-b1a4-4a6d17ca1d0f/smll202305686-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/41957dd4-f935-4bb8-abdf-6af1d492f4a1/smll202305686-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological characteristics of P-C-CNT microspheres: a) SEM image, b,c) TEM images, d) HR-TEM image, e) SAED pattern, and f) elemental mapping images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The newly developed carbon microspheres (P-C-CNT, D-C-CNT, and D-C) can be utilized as reservoir materials for metal or nonmetal components. <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2</a> provides a schematic illustration of the strategies employed to synthesize the porous microsphere with iron sulfide nanocrystals encapsulated in a highly porous carbon-coated CNT. In Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2a</a>, the prepared porous carbon-coated CNT porous microsphere (P-C-CNT) was impregnated with an infiltration solution containing iron nitrate. This solution deeply infiltrated the porous carbon surrounding the CNTs due to capillary forces, as shown in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2a</a>. The infiltrated powder (iron nitrate-P-C-CNT composite) was then uniformly mixed with sulfur nanopowders. Subsequently, the mixed sulfur powders were melted and infiltrated into a porous carbon channel during heat treatment, resulting in the formation and growth of iron sulfide nanocrystals within the developed porous voids through the Ostwald ripening effect as depicted in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2b</a>. This process led to the formation of a porous microsphere with iron sulfide nanocrystals encapsulated in a highly porous carbon-coated CNT (denoted as P-FeS@C-CNT). In contrast, when the same conditions for iron sulfide formation were applied to D-C-CNT and D-C the immediately formed iron sulfide nanocrystals were pushed outward and grew since the iron sulfide nanocrystals had no internal voids, as shown in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2c,d</a>. Consequently, comparative samples were formed with iron sulfide nanocrystals decorating highly porous carbon in two variations: one with CNT (referred to as D-FeS-C-CNT) and the other without (referred to as D-FeS-C). Notably, in the absence of CNT, the iron sulfide nanocrystals are prone to more significant Ostwald ripening, resulting in larger crystal sizes, as shown in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2d</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0011"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f81a7d41-b904-4e3d-8fd2-b967faa5806f/smll202305686-fig-0011-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f81a7d41-b904-4e3d-8fd2-b967faa5806f/smll202305686-fig-0011-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9a1e3426-0041-4611-828c-d1bc105d0714/smll202305686-fig-0011-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f81a7d41-b904-4e3d-8fd2-b967faa5806f/smll202305686-fig-0011-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0011&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of sulfidation processes of iron nitrate infiltrated P-C-CNT, D-C-CNT, and D-C: a) sulfidation process for the fabrication of small-sized iron sulfide nanocrystals encapsulated in P-C-CNT, b) formation mechanism of completely encapsulated iron sulfide nanocrystals in the inner voids of P-C-CNT. Sulfidation processes for the fabrication of larger-sized iron sulfide nanocrystals located on the outer surface of c) D-C-CNT and d) D-C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphological characteristics of P-FeS@C-CNT are illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2</a>. The SEM and TEM images in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2a</a> and&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2b</a> demonstrated that the spherical microsphere composed of iron sulfide nanocrystals, carbon, and CNTs exhibited non-aggregated properties. Notably, it was evident that the iron sulfide nanocrystals were almost completely absent on the surface of the microsphere, indicating their encapsulation within the porous carbon-coated CNT. The carbon shell, derived from PVP through phase segregation of PVP and sucrose, was clearly observed as indicated by the arrow in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2c</a>. The outer carbon shell, in addition to the space inside the microsphere, played a crucial role in preventing the escape of iron sulfide nanocrystals to the external environment. Within the porous inner voids, the iron sulfide nanocrystals were situated as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2d</a>. The high-resolution TEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2d</a> revealed distinct lattice fringes with separations of 0.30 and 0.27&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (110) and (112) crystal planes of Fe<sub>0.975</sub>S and FeS phases, respectively. The SAED pattern in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2e</a> confirmed the presence of Fe<sub>0.975</sub>S and FeS phases in P-FeS@C-CNT. The elemental mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2f</a> demonstrated that the iron sulfide nanocrystals were uniformly embedded within the porous carbon matrix. The presence of nitrogen, derived from the decomposition of PVP, further supported the formation of the N-doped carbon shell.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/af219525-57a1-40c1-9659-14d4a3cc4cdb/smll202305686-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/af219525-57a1-40c1-9659-14d4a3cc4cdb/smll202305686-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ef3a0e40-3e42-4bdb-a12b-a878efdd0083/smll202305686-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/af219525-57a1-40c1-9659-14d4a3cc4cdb/smll202305686-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological characteristics of P-FeS@C-CNT microspheres: a) SEM image, b,c) TEM images, d) HR-TEM image, e) SAED pattern, and f) elemental mapping images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphologies of D-FeS-C-CNT and D-FeS-C in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3</a> exhibited clear differences compared to those of P-FeS@C-CNT. In the SEM and TEM images of D-FeS-C-CNT shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, a grainy surface was observed on the microspheres, as indicated by arrows. This grainy surface consisted of iron sulfide nanocrystals that were formed by being pushed outward due to the absence of voids inside the microsphere, as a result of Ostwald ripening. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3c,d</a> simultaneously displayed small-sized nanocrystals embedded in the dense carbon microsphere and relatively larger-sized nanocrystals exposed on the surface. The HR-TEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3d</a> revealed distinct lattice fringes separated by 0.30 and 0.27&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (110) and (112) crystal planes of Fe<sub>0.975</sub>S and FeS phases, respectively. Additionally, the SAED pattern in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3e</a> confirmed the presence of Fe<sub>0.975</sub>S and FeS phases, indicating that both P-FeS@C-CNT and D-FeS-C-CNT shared the same crystal structure phase. The elemental mapping images also illustrated the presence of small-sized nanocrystals embedded in the dense carbon microsphere and relatively larger-sized nanocrystals exposed on the surface of D-FeS-C-CNT. The morphological characteristics of D-FeS-C (without the CNT component) were also shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3g–l</a>. Similar to D-FeS-C-CNT, a number of iron sulfide nanocrystals exposed on the surface were observed, as indicated by arrows in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3g,h</a>. Notably, the size of the exposed nanocrystals was relatively larger than that of D-FeS-C-CNT, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3i</a>. Interestingly, the PVP-derived carbon shell and sucrose-derived carbon core were clearly distinguishable, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3j</a>. Furthermore, in comparison to the D-FeS-C-CNT, a few iron sulfide nanocrystals embedded inside the carbon microsphere were observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3j</a>, suggesting that the presence of CNTs prevented the escape of iron sulfide through Ostwald ripening, as mentioned in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2d</a>. The HR-TEM and SAED pattern in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3j,k</a>, respectively, exhibited values identical to those of D-FeS-C-CNT. The elemental mapping images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3l</a> clearly demonstrated that the majority of iron sulfide nanocrystals were located on the outer surface of the microsphere.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f3294cc8-e0c4-4245-a431-a478799156ee/smll202305686-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f3294cc8-e0c4-4245-a431-a478799156ee/smll202305686-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/04e10812-0f19-48c9-92b6-442c5334cd8b/smll202305686-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f3294cc8-e0c4-4245-a431-a478799156ee/smll202305686-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological characteristics of a–f) D-FeS-C-CNT and g–l) D-FeS-C microspheres: a,g) SEM images, b,c,h,i) TEM images, d,j) HR-TEM images, e,k) SAED patterns, and f,l) elemental mapping images.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In this study, the effect of encapsulating iron sulfide nanocrystals through the sulfidation method was investigated as described in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4</a>. Three different strategies were employed to convert iron nitrate into iron sulfide. The first method involved metal salt infiltration, where a solution containing iron nitrate was impregnated into the P-C-CNT reservoir. The infiltrated powders were then mixed with sulfur powders and reacted at high temperatures to form iron sulfide (as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4a</a>). The iron nitrate solution was able to penetrate the small inner pores of P-C-CNT through capillary action, thanks to its rich pore volume. Upon reaction with uniformly mixed sulfur, the initially melted sulfur penetrated additional pores in P-C-CNT, leading to the formation of iron sulfide nanocrystals within the inner pores. As a result, the iron nitrate-infiltrated P-C-CNT was successfully transformed into a porous microsphere with encapsulated iron sulfide nanocrystals, as illustrated in Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0011">2</a>. SEM images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information confirmed the absence of exposed nanocrystals on the microsphere surface, supporting the successful encapsulation. The second method (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4c</a>) involved metal and nonmetal co-infiltration, where a solution containing iron nitrate and thiourea (a sulfur source) was impregnated into the P-C-CNT reservoir. However, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information, crystal growth emerged from the microsphere surface, indicating that the iron sulfide crystals could not be sufficiently encapsulated within P-C-CNT. It can be inferred that during heat treatment when the impregnating solution was thoroughly absorbed into P-C-CNT, the melting of thiourea coincided with the elution of iron nitrate particles. Consequently, the encapsulation process was compromised. Similar to the second method, the third method resulted in a morphology comparable to that of the second method. This third method involved impregnating P-C-CNT with iron nitrate and subsequently heat-treating it under an H<sub>2</sub>S gas atmosphere as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4e</a>. The crystallization of iron sulfides occurred mainly from the outer regions of the P-C-CNT microsphere due to the presence of H<sub>2</sub>S gas. Consequently, the iron sulfide nanocrystals were expelled from the microsphere through Ostwald ripening, as demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0004">4f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7594db35-22c3-4738-b324-b233020bcaa4/smll202305686-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7594db35-22c3-4738-b324-b233020bcaa4/smll202305686-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d28c62f1-4fe3-4282-80b4-51f13b2bc6af/smll202305686-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7594db35-22c3-4738-b324-b233020bcaa4/smll202305686-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of various sulfidation methods and SEM images of FeS-C-CNT composite microspheres fabricated by following processes: a) sulfidation of iron-nitrate infiltrated powders mixed with sulfur nanopowders, b) SEM image, c) sulfidation of powders infiltrated with mixture of iron-nitrate and thiourea, d) SEM image, e) sulfidation of iron-nitrate infiltrated powders under H<sub>2</sub>S gas atmosphere, and f) SEM image.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The crystal structures of P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-CNT, and D-FeS-C were examined using X-ray diffraction (XRD), as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0005">5a</a>. All three samples exhibited the same crystal structures, specifically Pyrrhotite Fe<sub>0.975</sub>S (PDF#80-1032) and Troilite FeS (PDF#80-1027) solid solution. To further investigate the chemical bonding states in P-FeS@C-CNT, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was performed, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0005">5b–e</a>. The high-resolution Fe 2p XPS spectra displayed two distinct peaks corresponding to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. The peaks were fitted at 712.4 and 725.3&nbsp;eV, indicating Fe<sup>2+</sup> (related to Fe<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>S) and 712.4 and 725.3&nbsp;eV for Fe<sup>3+</sup> (related to Fe-SO<i><sub>x</sub></i>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0027_R_d995774e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Res.">27</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0028_R_d995774e923" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Power Sources">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0005">5c</a>, the high-resolution S 2p spectra exhibited four categories: S<sup>2−</sup>, S<i><sub>n</sub></i><sup>2−</sup>, SO<i><sub>x</sub></i>, and C–S. The presence of S<sup>2−</sup> peaks indicated the presence of iron sulfides, while the S<i><sub>n</sub></i><sup>2−</sup> peaks suggested the existence of iron polysulfide species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0029_R_d995774e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Energy Lett.">29</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0030_R_d995774e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. – Eur. J.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The SO<i><sub>x</sub></i> peak corresponded to the partial oxidation of FeS during sample preparation under an air atmosphere, aligning with the Fe<sup>3+</sup> peaks observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0005">5b</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0029_R_d995774e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Energy Lett.">29</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0031_R_d995774e961" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Front Chem">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the C–S bonding peaks (located at 165.5 and 163.6&nbsp;eV) indicated the presence of iron sulfide and carbon composites in P-FeS@C-CNT.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0032_R_d995774e967" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Commun.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C 1s spectra of P-FeS@C-CNT in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0005">5d</a> were fitted to five peaks at 284.6, 285.5, 285.8, 286.1, and 288.7&nbsp;eV, corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup>-bonded carbon (C–C), N-doped carbon (C–N), C-S bonded carbon (C–S), epoxy and alkoxy groups (C–O), and carbonyl and carboxylic (O–C=O) components, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0033_R_d995774e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Ionics">33</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0034_R_d995774e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of N-doped carbon in PVP-derived carbon is a characteristic reported in previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0021_R_d995774e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small Methods">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The N 1s spectrum in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0005">5e</a>, originating from N-doped carbon, was deconvoluted into three peaks at 398.3, 400.9, and 402.0&nbsp;eV, representing pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, and graphitic N, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0033_R_d995774e991" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Ionics">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Raman spectra of the three samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) commonly exhibited two prominent peaks at 1342 and 1592 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which were assigned to disordered carbon (D-band) and graphitic carbon (G-band), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0035_R_d995774e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">35</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0036_R_d995774e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nano–Micro Lett.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicated the presence of crystalline nature in the PVP- and sucrose-derived carbon and CNTs in all three samples. Thermogravimetry (TG) data of the three samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) revealed similar weight loss patterns due to the conversion of iron sulfide to iron oxide and the combustion of the carbon component. Initially, all three samples showed weight increase up to 400 °C, which was attributed to the formation of iron sulfate through partial oxidation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0027_R_d995774e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Res.">27</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0037_R_d995774e1010" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nanoscale">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> P-FeS@C-CNT and D-FeS-C-CNT exhibited a two-step weight loss: the first step was associated with the decomposition of carbon derived from PVP and sucrose as well as the oxidation of iron sulfide and iron sulfate, while the second step corresponded to the decomposition of CNTs. The decomposition of CNTs occurred at higher temperatures than the carbons derived from PVP and sucrose. In the case of D-FeS-C without CNTs, a single-step weight reduction was observed. The calculated carbon content in the three samples was 56 wt%, assuming a 1:1 weight ratio for Fe<sub>0.975</sub>S and FeS.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a2eee00b-f978-47db-99b5-ecf0ec8c4f74/smll202305686-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a2eee00b-f978-47db-99b5-ecf0ec8c4f74/smll202305686-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0f47d483-a2e2-4cb1-a232-71ae3e6f03f0/smll202305686-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a2eee00b-f978-47db-99b5-ecf0ec8c4f74/smll202305686-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD patterns of three iron sulfide-carbon composites and XPS spectra of P-FeS@C-CNT: a) XRD patterns, b) Fe 2p spectrum, c) S 2p spectrum, d) C 1s spectrum, and e) N 1s spectrum.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical reaction mechanism of the iron sulfide-carbon composite samples was investigated using cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements. The CV measurements were conducted within a potential range of ≈0.001 to 3.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, as depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0006">6</a>. The three samples (P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C) exhibited similar cathodic and anodic peaks during repeated cycling. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0006">6a</a>, the P-FeS@C-CNT electrode exhibited an irreversible reduction peak at around 1.4&nbsp;V at the initial discharging process. Compared to the D-FeS-C electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0006">6c</a>), this peak could be attributed to the irreversible reaction between the sodium ion and the functional groups of the CNT component.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0038_R_d995774e1056" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Sci.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, all three electrodes showed broad and small peaks at ≈1.5 to 2.25&nbsp;V during the initial discharging process. These broad peaks arise from the intercalation of sodium ions into iron sulfide nanocrystals at high voltage to form the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0039_R_d995774e1064" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, despite the same contents of CNT components in both P-FeS@C-CNT and D-FeS-C-CNT, the D-FeS-C-CNT electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0006">6b</a>) exhibited an almost negligible peak, which was attributed to the CNT component (1.4&nbsp;V). It could be due to the differences in the morphological characteristics of these electrodes. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2c</a>, the CNT component of the P-C-CNT microsphere was exposed on the surface due to the complete encapsulation of iron sulfide nanocrystals. Conversely, the CNT component of D-FeS-C-CNT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3c</a>) was embedded in the dense carbon microsphere, and the large iron sulfide nanocrystals were exposed on the surface. Consequently, the irreversible reaction between the sodium ion and the functional groups of the CNT component within the D-FeS-C-CNT electrode was considerably limited. After these processes (≈1.5 to 2.25 and 1.4&nbsp;V), two other prominent peaks were observed in all three electrodes. These peaks were related to the conversion from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>FeS to metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as the generation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layers (0.7&nbsp;V), and the intercalation of sodium ions into the carbon components (0.01&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0039_R_d995774e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0040_R_d995774e1085" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the initial discharge reaction can be expressed as follows.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305686-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bbccfdaf-dba2-4eac-bd49-93e814330978/smll202305686-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202305686:smll202305686-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202305686-math-0001.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>FeS</mi>
                              <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                           </mrow>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mo>+</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>e</mi>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <mo>→</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>Na</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi>Fe</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{FeS \; + }}\ 2{\mathrm{Na}}^ + + \ 2{e}^ - \ \to {\mathrm{Na}}_2{\mathrm{S}} + {\mathrm{Fe}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d8d1c9bd-ebe2-4c27-8282-4743fa092ba1/smll202305686-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d8d1c9bd-ebe2-4c27-8282-4743fa092ba1/smll202305686-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ec07c2ac-f250-4ddc-b013-1d1032abd517/smll202305686-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d8d1c9bd-ebe2-4c27-8282-4743fa092ba1/smll202305686-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves of a) P-FeS@C-CNT, b) D-FeS-C-CNT, and c) D-FeS-C at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">During the subsequent charging process, two anodic peaks at 1.45 and 1.82&nbsp;V were observed, attributed to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>FeS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>2−x</sub>FeS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0039_R_d995774e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0040_R_d995774e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All three samples demonstrated good reversibility during the initial five cycles. Based on this observation, the possible reaction mechanisms subsequent to the initial discharging are listed as follows.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0039_R_d995774e1165" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">39</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0040_R_d995774e1165" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305686-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">During the initial discharging and charging processes, the changes in resistance and sodium ion diffusion coefficients of the three samples (P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C) were compared using in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7</a>. The Nyquist plots, which were measured at specific potentials during the initial discharging and charging process, were collected and summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7a–c</a>. These plots included contributions from the solution resistance (<i>R<sub>s</sub></i>), the interfacial layer associated with the SEI layer (<i>R<sub>sei</sub></i>), and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>). The variation of the total resistance (<i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> = <i>R<sub>s</sub></i> + <i>R<sub>sei</sub></i> + <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>) during the initial cycle, obtained by in situ EIS at preselected potentials, was represented by corresponding colored dots in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7d–f</a>. All three samples exhibited a significant decrease in <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> during the initial discharging process until ≈1.0&nbsp;V. This decrease could be attributed to the initial sodiation process and the aging of the sodium ions, regardless of the sufficient aging time (6&nbsp;h). Subsequently, phase transition, volume expansion due to the conversion reaction, and the formation of SEI layers (as confirmed by CV curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0006">6</a>) led to an increase in electrode resistance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0041_R_d995774e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Chem. Eng. J.">41</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0042_R_d995774e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Contrary to expectations, the <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> values showed a slight decrease until the discharge was completed. This can be attributed to the transformation of large iron sulfide nanocrystals into ultrafine nanocrystals during the sodiation process, which had a larger effect on the total resistance of the electrode than the aforementioned reasons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0043_R_d995774e1306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano–Micro Lett.">43</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0044_R_d995774e1306" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano-Micro Lett.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the end of the initial discharge, the <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> values for P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C were 232, 369, and 271 Ω, respectively. The low resistance value of P-FeS@C-CNT can be attributed to its porous structure, small-sized iron sulfide nanocrystals, and the uniform composite with CNTs. Despite the presence of a conductive CNT component in D-FeS-C-CNT, its <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> value is larger than that of D-FeS-C after the initial discharge. As observed from the morphological characteristics in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0003">3c,i</a>, the iron sulfide nanocrystals in D-FeS-C-CNT were relatively located inside the core compared to D-FeS-C. P-FeS@C-CNT with a porous structure and D-FeS-C with exposed iron sulfide nanocrystals on the surface enables rapid electrolyte penetration and contact. This facilitates a fast conversion reaction from Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS to metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as fast diffusion of sodium ions during the initial sodiation process. During the subsequent charging process, the <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> of all three samples continuously decreased, possibly due to the volume contraction as well as the disappearance of the non-conductive Na<sub>2</sub>S matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0045_R_d995774e1334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Chem. Eng. J.">45</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0046_R_d995774e1334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Eng. J.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> values for P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C at the end of the initial charge were 172, 209, and 193 Ω, respectively. The sodium ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) variations of the three samples, calculated using the following equation in the Nyquist plot, also showed good correlations with the <i>R</i><sub><i>tot</i></sub> variations in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7d–f</a>.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305686-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b41035c8-f5de-479d-ae1a-74639bfccfcb/smll202305686-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202305686:smll202305686-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/smll202305686-math-0004.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>

                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </msup>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mn>0.5</mn>
                           <msup>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                                 <mi>RT</mi>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mi>S</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>n</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi>F</mi>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </msup>
