                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {D}_{{\rm Na}^{+}}=0.5(\textit{RT}/S{n}^{2}{F}^{2}C\sigma)^{2} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6905a43e-242c-42bb-a6f4-82fd841bbbdd/smll202305686-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6905a43e-242c-42bb-a6f4-82fd841bbbdd/smll202305686-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a1f978f8-895e-4603-a402-d174aa8fb4e2/smll202305686-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6905a43e-242c-42bb-a6f4-82fd841bbbdd/smll202305686-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) In situ EIS Nyquist plots of P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C measured at preselected potentials during the first cycle, d–f) in situ EIS graphs (<i>R</i><sub>tot</sub> vs potential), and g-i) variations in sodium-ion diffusion coefficients calculated from in situ EIS results. j–l) In situ EIS Nyquist plots of P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C measured at preselected potentials after 100 cycles, m–o) in situ EIS graphs (<i>R</i><sub>tot</sub> vs potential), and p–r) variations in sodium-ion diffusion coefficients calculated from in-situ EIS results.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>A detailed description of parameter values can be found in the previous literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0047_R_d995774e1418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Eng. J.">47</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0048_R_d995774e1418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the previous literature, the intercalation of sodium ions into iron sulfide nanocrystals (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS) and hard carbon may lead to a reduction in sodium ion diffusivity, leaving fewer sites available for additional sodium ions to diffuse into.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0049_R_d995774e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0050_R_d995774e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the phase transition of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS to metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S enhances the sodium ion diffusivity, which is ascribed to the low crystallinity of metallic Fe and weaker ionic bonds of Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0042_R_d995774e1439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0051_R_d995774e1439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering these factors and the gradual increase in diffusion coefficient values during the sodiation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7g–i</a>), it can be seen that the phase transition of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS to metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S had a larger effect on the total sodium-ion diffusivity of the electrodes, regardless of the high contents of carbon components in these electrodes. Consequently, the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values of the three samples gradually decrease as the charging process progresses. It is worth noting that the lowest diffusivity observed in D-FeS-C-CNT can be attributed to the nanocrystals located within the densely structured carbon material. To further investigate the influence of FeS-C nanostructures on resistance and sodium ion diffusivity after cycling, in situ EIS measurements were performed after 100 cycles, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7j–r</a>. Comparing the results with the initial cycle, the charge transfer kinetics and sodium ion diffusivity of the three samples improved due to the activation process in the initial few cycles. Furthermore, unlike the <i>R</i><sub><i>tot</i></sub> variations at the initial discharge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7d–f</a>), the <i>R</i><sub><i>tot</i></sub> values after 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7m–o</a>) were gradually increased during the discharge process, which was mainly ascribed to the volume expansion. This suggests that the decrease in <i>R</i><sub><i>tot</i></sub> values observed during the initial discharge (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7d–f</a>) could be attributed to the formation of ultrafine nanocrystals due to the conversion reaction as well as the initial activation of the hard carbon component.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0043_R_d995774e1482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano–Micro Lett.">43</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0044_R_d995774e1482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano-Micro Lett.">44</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0052_R_d995774e1482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Carbon Energy">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, in contrast to the first cycle, P-FeS@C-CNT exhibited superior charge transfer and sodium ion diffusion kinetics compared to the other two samples. This can be attributed to the small-sized nanocrystals and porous structure of P-FeS@C-CNT, which effectively accommodates the significant volume changes during repeated cycling. On the other hand, D-FeS-C-CNT and D-FeS-C, with their dense structures, are prone to structural damage, resulting in sluggish charge transfer and diffusion kinetics after cycling. The fracturing and pulverization of FeS-C particles, along with the formation of thick SEI layers, likely contribute to this behavior. Interestingly, despite the morphological characteristics mentioned earlier for D-FeS-C-CNT, it exhibited faster charge transfer and sodium ion diffusivity than D-FeS-C after 100 cycles. This indicates that the nanocrystals within the carbon structure effectively mitigate the stress caused by significant volume changes during cycling. The in situ EIS data after 150 cycles also demonstrate the excellent cycling stability of P-FeS@C-CNT, as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information. The degradation trend in charge transfer kinetics and sodium ion diffusivity, as influenced by the morphological characteristics of the three samples, exhibits similar behavior as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7j–r</a>. Notably, despite D-FeS-C having the highest average <i>R</i><sub><i>tot</i></sub> value due to structural instability, the severe pulverization of D-FeS-C helps alleviate the stress resulting from volume expansion during the sodiation process. Consequently, D-FeS-C shows the smallest variation in <i>R</i><sub><i>tot</i></sub>, as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11f</a>, Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the three samples as anodes for sodium-ion batteries was evaluated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8</a>. The initial galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of the three samples at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> showed good fitting to the redox peaks observed in the CV curves, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8a</a>. P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C exhibited initial discharge capacities of 1027, 888, and 845&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with initial coulombic efficiencies (ICE) of 39%, 45%, and 43%, respectively. The presence of a porous structure in P-FeS@C-CNT led to higher reactivity and increased formation of SEI layers, resulting in a relatively lower ICE value. The rate capabilities of the three samples were compared, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8b</a>. At current densities of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, P-FeS@C-CNT exhibited reversible discharge capacities of 407, 345, 284, 210, 162, 126, and 100&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, and the capacity returned to 432&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density was reduced back to 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The discharge capacities of D-FeS-C-CNT and D-FeS-C at a current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> showed slightly higher values of 118 and 132&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Up to a relatively low current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, P-FeS@C-CNT exhibited slightly higher rate capabilities. However, at high current densities between 2 and 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it demonstrated relatively lower rate capabilities compared to the other two samples. This suggests that at high current densities, nanocrystals exposed on the surface of the carbon shell contribute more to the rate performance than those located inside porous carbon structures. On the other hand, considering cycle stability, the encapsulated nanocrystals exhibited superior performance compared to the exposed nanocrystals. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information, the subsequent cycle performances of the three samples at a current density of 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup> indicate the stable cycling of P-FeS@C-CNT for 200 cycles, while the other two samples exhibited continuous capacity fading after 120 cycles. The in situ EIS data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0007">7j–r</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information confirm that the iron sulfide nanocrystals encapsulated in the porous carbon structure are beneficial in accommodating volume stress during cycling, resulting in excellent cycling stability. Consequently, the cycle performances of the three samples in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8c</a> also demonstrate the superior cycle stability of P-FeS@C-CNT, with a capacity retention of 117% compared to the 2<sup>nd</sup> cycle. From the 2<sup>nd</sup> to the 200<sup>th</sup> cycle, the discharge capacity of P-FeS@C-CNT further increased from 370 to 432&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> due to increased capacity during the initial 20 cycles. The initial capacity increase of P-FeS@C-CNT is commonly attributed to the formation of a polymeric gel-like film, which corresponds to the reversible formation of SEI layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0053_R_d995774e1565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">53</a>, <a href="#smll202305686-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0054_R_d995774e1565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the discharge capacities of D-FeS-C-CNT and D-FeS-C after 200 cycles were 257 and 107&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. To get the actual sodium-ion storage of the carbon component in the P-FeS@C-CNT, the cycle tests of bare P-C-CNT anode for SIBs were conducted as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information. In this study, the porous carbon components surrounding the CNTs, which were derived from the decomposition of PVP and sucrose under 1000&nbsp;°C, had characteristics of amorphous carbon and some portions of hard carbon. Therefore, the initial Coulombic efficiency of P-C-CNT showed a very low value (14%), which was attributed to the irreversible absorption of sodium ion to the functional group over P-C-CNT and the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film within the micropores inside or on the surface of the P-C-CNT. The initial discharge capacity of P-C-CNT was 700&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a gradual decrease in discharge capacity from 140 to 104&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was observed between the 2<sup>nd</sup> and the 200<sup>th</sup> cycles. Considering carbon contents in the P-FeS@C-CNT, which was proved in the TG data (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), the actual capacity contributed by carbon in P-FeS@C-CNT after 200<sup>th</sup> cycle is calculated to 58&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, P-FeS@C-CNT exhibited excellent cycling performance even at a high current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8d</a>. In the long-term cycling, P-FeS@C-CNT maintained a reversible discharge capacity of 293&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for 600 cycles, showing improved electrochemical performance compared to previously reported iron sulfide-carbon composite anode materials for SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The ex situ EIS measurements conducted before cycling and after 100 cycles also demonstrated the excellent cycle stability of P-FeS@C-CNT. Prior to cycling, the P-FeS@C-CNT electrode exhibited the smallest <i>R</i><sub>tot</sub> values among the three electrodes, indicating that the porous carbon structure of P-FeS@C-CNT facilitates fast electrolyte penetration and boosts the aging process of sodium ions. Additionally, the presence of additional semicircles after 100 cycles indicates the formation of SEI layers, with <i>R</i><sub>tot</sub> values of 64, 78, and 80 Ω for P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C, respectively. The higher <i>R<sub>tot</sub></i> values of D-FeS-C-CNT and D-FeS-C can be attributed to the formation of thick SEI layers resulting from the continuous structural destruction during repeated cycling. This observation is further supported by the SEM images of the cycled electrodes, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information. P-FeS@C-CNT electrode exhibits excellent stability even after 150 cycles, while D-FeS-C-CNT and D-FeS-C electrodes exhibit severe structural breaking.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3ca02f42-0397-46a3-a633-44eeabbec297/smll202305686-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3ca02f42-0397-46a3-a633-44eeabbec297/smll202305686-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/00cdc784-05fa-4142-9b10-d4d0ed38c352/smll202305686-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3ca02f42-0397-46a3-a633-44eeabbec297/smll202305686-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C electrodes: a) initial discharge/charge curves, b) rate performances, c) cycle performances at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Long term cycling performance of P-FeS@C-CNT at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with activation firstly at a cycling rate of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for five times. Nyquist plots of e) fresh cells and f) after 100 cycles, g) SEM images of P-FeS@C-CNT after 150 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Apart from the stable cycling performance of P-FeS@C-CNT, the low ICE value (39%), attributed to the high carbon content (56&nbsp;wt.% calculated from TG data), requires improvement for practical application. In this study, the P-FeS@C-CNT was prepared by infiltration of solution dissolved by 4&nbsp;g of iron nitrate into the 1&nbsp;g of porous carbon microsphere powders. The carbon content of P-FeS@C-CNT could be determined by the infiltration amount of iron nitrate into porous carbon microspheres (P-C-CNT). Therefore, to reduce the carbon content in the P-FeS@C-CNT, a higher infiltration amount of iron nitrate is required. To investigate the optimal infiltration amount of iron nitrate into P-C-CNT, excess (6 and 8&nbsp;g) infiltration amount of iron nitrate was applied to 1&nbsp;g of P-C-CNT. The SEM images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b–e</a>, Supporting Information revealed obvious morphological differences according to infiltration amount of iron nitrate. In case of optimal infiltration amount (4&nbsp;g) of iron nitrate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0002">2a</a>), the iron sulfide nanocrystals were completely encapsulated within the P-C-CNT. On the other hand, in case of excess infiltration amount (6 and 8&nbsp;g) of iron nitrate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b–e</a>, Supporting Information), the iron sulfide nanocrystals were simultaneously observed inside and outside the carbon shell. Additionally, it was found that an increased infiltration of iron nitrate led to a greater exposure of iron sulfide nanocrystals to the outside of the carbon shell. When the infiltration amount was exceeded, the iron nitrate was not sufficiently infiltrated into the pore space of P-C-CNT, resulting in the formation of iron sulfide nanocrystals outside the carbon shell. Furthermore, the electrochemical properties of FeS-C-CNT electrodes, which were formed by excess infiltration amount (6 and 8&nbsp;g) of iron nitrate, were investigated as anode materials for SIBs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to the P-FeS@C-CNT (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8a</a>), the electrodes, which were formed by excess infiltration amount (6 and 8&nbsp;g) of iron nitrate, exhibited higher ICE values (49% and 54%, respectively) due to the lower carbon contents. However, this improvement was accompanied by an unstable cyclic performance due to the structural instability of iron sulfide nanocrystals exposed outside the carbon shell. The development of a carbon substrate with controlled pores capable of accommodating a higher amount of infiltrated iron nitrate would facilitate the high ICE. From this perspective, this study has highlighted the potential for adjusting the porosity of carbon materials by controlling the sucrose content in the spray solution. This indicates significant potential for improvement.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To explore the electrochemical kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> storage in the three electrodes, CV rate tests were conducted at different scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305686-fig-0009">9</a>, and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). The relationship between the scan rate (<i>v</i>) and the measured current (<i>i</i>) can be described by the following equation, as depicted in the CV curves at various scan rates.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305686-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/692bef8c-7a87-481c-9d49-06aae416d64e/smll202305686-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202305686:smll202305686-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/smll202305686-math-0005.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>i</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>v</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mi>log</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>a</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{log}}\left( i \right) = b{\mathrm{log}}\left( v \right) + {\mathrm{log}}\left( a \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202305686-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e8cfe514-8f0c-4b66-bd74-f17ccd090c24/smll202305686-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e8cfe514-8f0c-4b66-bd74-f17ccd090c24/smll202305686-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a0b278c0-fe38-4242-a79f-0ac2bfcfe87a/smll202305686-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e8cfe514-8f0c-4b66-bd74-f17ccd090c24/smll202305686-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305686-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305686" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of P-FeS@C-CNT at various scan rates, b) fitted log (peak current) versus log (scan rate) for peaks 1 and 2, c) CV curves of P-FeS@C-CNT showing capacitive contribution (green colored area) to the total current, and d) capacity contribution at different scan rates of P-FeS@C-CNT.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The b values corresponding to the redox peaks in the three electrodes were determined from log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) plots, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0009">9b</a>, and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18b</a>, Supporting Information. It can be observed that all three electrodes exhibited high b values during both the reduction (peak 1) and oxidation (peak 2) processes, indicating significant capacitive behavior of the iron sulfide nanocrystals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0055_R_d995774e1751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Mater. Chem. A">55</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0057_R_d995774e1751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Small">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This capacitive behavior likely contributes to the excellent rate capabilities observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0008">8b</a>. To further understand the contribution of capacitive capacity and diffusion-controlled capacity, the following equation was used for investigation.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305686-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a69c3782-13a3-4e1c-9daf-428b44a250c1/smll202305686-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202305686:smll202305686-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/smll202305686-math-0006.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                              <mi>V</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msub>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                                 <mo>/</mo>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </mrow>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i\left( V \right) = {k}_1v + {k}_2{v}^{1/2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>The parameters <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the surface capacitive and diffusion-controlled behaviors, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305686-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0055_R_d995774e1800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Mater. Chem. A">55</a>-<a href="#smll202305686-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305686-bib-0057_R_d995774e1800" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Small">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At a scan rate of 1.5&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the percentage of capacitive contribution (indicated by the green area) in P-FeS@C-CNT, D-FeS-C-CNT, and D-FeS-C electrodes was found to be 72%, 70%, and 71%, respectively, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305686-fig-0009">9d</a>, and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18d</a>, Supporting Information. These capacitive contributions exhibited a gradual increase with higher scan rates, further indicating the excellent rate capabilities of the three electrodes.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305686-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305686-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this study, we propose the use of a highly porous carbon-coated CNT microsphere as an exceptional reservoir for sodium-ion batteries. Through a simple infiltration process followed by sulfidation, we successfully prepared a porous microsphere with iron sulfide nanocrystals encapsulated within a porous carbon structure. The use of porous carbon with multiple voids and an optimized sulfidation method ensures complete encapsulation of the iron sulfide nanocrystals. Importantly, the encapsulation of the nanocrystals within the porous carbon structure provides a beneficial solution to accommodate the significant volume changes that occur during cycling. Additionally, the uniform dispersion of CNTs throughout the composite greatly enhances its electrical conductivity. As a result, the porous FeS-C-CNT composite microsphere exhibits robust cycling stability and high rate capabilities when employed as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries. This rational encapsulation strategy offers a promising approach to overcome the challenges associated with volume change and low conductivity in various applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305686-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305686-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>The highly porous carbon-coated CNT microspheres were prepared by spray pyrolysis, selenization, and etching processes. During the spray pyrolysis process, droplets were generated using a 1.7-MHz ultrasonic spray generator consisting of six vibrators. The droplets were carried to a quartz reactor with a length of 1200&nbsp;mm and diameter of 50&nbsp;mm maintained at 400 °C using air as a carrier gas at a flow rate of 10 L min<sup>−1</sup>. The spray solution was prepared by dissolving 0.2&nbsp;M of Sn(II) oxalate, 0.05&nbsp;M of magnesium nitrate hexahydrate, 8&nbsp;g L<sup>−1</sup> of PVP (<i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 40 000), a certain concentration of sucrose with a PVP/sucrose weight ratio of 1/2, and 1.6&nbsp;mg ml<sup>−1</sup> of acid treated multi-wall CNTs (MWCNTs) in 1 L of distilled water. The acid-treated MWCNTs were obtained by oxidizing MWCNTs through treatment with an HNO<sub>3</sub>/H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> (1:3 v/v) solution at 70&nbsp;°C, followed by washing with distilled water. As a comparative sample, the porous carbon-coated CNT dense microspheres were prepared by increasing the sucrose concentration in the spray solution under the same conditions. The amount of sucrose added was determined to be a PVP/sucrose weight ratio of 1/8. The sprayed precursor powders were post-treated in a tube furnace at 1000 °C under a 10% H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere for 3&nbsp;h and etched by HCl/HNO<sub>3</sub> solution to produce porous carbon-coated CNT microspheres. Based on 1&nbsp;g of porous carbon-coated microsphere powders, 4&nbsp;g of iron nitrate nonahydrate (Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O, Samchun Chemical Co. Ltd.) was dissolved in a high-purity solution of ethyl alcohol. The solution was infiltrated into the porous carbon-coated CNT microsphere powders. The infiltrated powders were thoroughly blended with sulfur nanopowders, maintaining an 8:1 molar ratio of sulfur to iron. This mixture then underwent a sulfidation process at 155 °C (12&nbsp;h) and 400 °C (6&nbsp;h) under a 10% H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere to form FeS-C-CNT composite microspheres. Detailed information pertaining to the characterization and electrochemical measurements of the prepared samples is provided in Supporting Information.</p>
         </section>
